Invalid Gift After Assignment of Dower
Ruling on wife's gift of dower after hawalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a wife's gift of dower after assigning it to a creditor and distinguish between specific and absolute assignment.

Core Idea
An assignment attaches a creditor's right like a pawn, blocking the wife's gift of the dower.

This passage presents a specific scenario: a wife owes a debt, assigns her creditor to collect from the dower owed by her husband, then gifts the dower back to the husband. The ruling clarifies that the gift is invalid because the creditor's right is attached. Understanding this helps grasp how a transfer of a debt right can restrict property dispositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the legal acts
Identify the wife (assignor/debtor), husband (debtor of dower), and creditor (assignee). The acts: hawalah (assignment) and hibah (gift). The assignment is from the wife to the creditor regarding the dower.
The husband owes the wife a dower; the wife owes a creditor; she tells the creditor to collect the dower from the husband.
2
Trace the sequence of events
Step 1: Wife assigns creditor to husband to collect dower. Step 2: Wife then gifts the dower to husband. The order matters.
If the gift came first, then assignment, the ruling might differ? But the passage only discusses this order.
3
Identify the key legal concept: attached right
After assignment, the creditor gains a right in the dower similar to a pawn (rahn). This means the dower becomes tied to the creditor's claim, so the wife cannot freely dispose of it by gift.
The analogy to rahn: a pawned item cannot be sold or gifted by the owner without the pawnee's consent.
4
Distinguish between specific and absolute assignment
If the assignment is 'by her dower' (specific), the gift is invalid. If the assignment is absolute (not specifying the dower), the gift is valid because no conflict arises.
The passage says: 'If she made an absolute assignment then gifted the dower, it is valid'.
5
State the ruling and its basis
Ruling: The gift is invalid when assignment is specifically on the dower. Basis: The assignee's right is attached to the dower, so the wife's disposition is void.
The ruling is from Hanafi fiqh and is a straightforward application of the principle that assignment creates a security interest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أحالت إنسانا على الزوج، ثم وهبت المهر للزوج لا تصح. صورة المسألة: أنه كان على المرأة دين لإنسان، فأحالت المرأة الدائن على زوجها بأن يقبض منه مهرها في دينه ثم وهبت المهر للزوج، لا تصح الهبة؛ لأنه تعلق به حق المحتال على مثال الرهن، وإن كان أسوة الغرماء عند موتها، فيرد تصرفها فيه، فصار كما باع المرهون أو وهبه هذا إذا أحالت بمهرها، ثم وهبت مهرها، أما إذا أطلقت الحوالة ثم وهبت المهر صح؛ إذ لا منافاة.
And if she made an assignment (hiyala) of a person to the husband, then gifted the dower to the husband, it is not valid. The form of the issue: There was a debt owed by the woman to a person, so she made an assignment of the creditor to her husband to receive her dower from him in payment of the debt, then she gifted the dower to the husband; the gift is not valid because the right of the assignee (creditor) is attached to it like a pawn (rahn), even if he is like other creditors at her death. So her disposition is void, as if she sold or gifted something pawned. This is if she assigned by her dower, then gifted her dower. But if she made an absolute assignment (not specifying the dower) then gifted the dower, it is valid; because no contradiction.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This ruling presents a case where a woman has a debt, and she assigns (hiyala) her creditor to her husband, instructing the husband to pay the creditor from her dower (mahr) which the husband owes her. Later, after the assignment, she gifts the dower back to her husband. The ruling states that the gift is invalid. The reason is that by the assignment, the creditor acquires a right in the dower, similar to a right of a pledgee in a pawned item. Even though the creditor is merely an unsecured creditor (like other creditors at death), the assignment creates a specific right in that dower. Therefore, the wife cannot dispose of it by gift. However, if she made a general assignment (hawalah mutlaqah) without specifying the dower, then the gift of the dower is valid because there is no conflict.

Key Terms
حوالة (hawalah): Assignment of a debt, transferring the right to collect from the debtor to another person
رهن (rahn): Pawn, pledge
مهر (mahr): Dower
هبة (hibah): Gift

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a woman assigns her creditor to collect her dower (specific assignment) and then gifts the dower to her husband?
A) The gift is valid because she still owns the dower.
B) The gift is invalid because the husband now owes the creditor instead.
C) The gift is invalid because the assignee's right is attached to the dower like a pawn.
D) The gift is valid only if the husband agrees.

Key Takeaway
An assignment of a specific debt (hawalah) attaches the creditor's right to that asset, blocking the debtor from later gifting it; only an absolute assignment leaves the asset freely disposable.

Imam's Authority to Allocate a Public Road
Ruling with proof on Imam's power over public roads

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the Imam's power to allocate a public road and explain the condition of no harm and the basis in istihsan.

Core Idea
The Imam may allocate public road land if it serves public benefit without harming anyone.

This lesson explores a key principle of public authority in Hanafi fiqh: the Imam (ruler) may dispose of public property like roads for the common good, as long as no harm results. The ruling is justified through istihsan (juristic preference) and supported by analogical reasoning. Understanding this helps clarify limits of state power over public assets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The text states that the Imam may 'cut' (allocate) a person from the main road. This means granting a portion of the public road to an individual for use, e.g., for building.
The verb 'يقطع' in this context means allocation (iqta'), not physical cutting.
2
Identify the Condition
The allocation is permitted only 'if it does not harm the passersby' (إن لم يضر بالمارة). This is the sole explicit condition.
Harm to the public is the overriding limitation on the Imam's discretion.
3
Recognize the Proof (Dalil)
The proof is the Imam's 'wilāyat al-taṣarruf' – authority to act in the collective interest of Muslims. This is a form of istihsan: the public benefit outweighs the individual's right to the road's original state.
Istihsan allows departure from strict analogy (qiyas) when a stronger public interest exists.
4
Examine the Analogical Support
The text cites the example of adding part of a road into a mosque or vice versa for Muslim benefit. This analogy reinforces that the Imam may alter public spaces for good.
The analogy shows the ruling is not arbitrary but follows a consistent principle.
5
Understand the Authority Level
The appointed Imam (by the caliph) is like the caliph himself; he is a deputy (na’ib). Thus, his authority in public matters is equivalent.
In later Hanafi terminology, this Imam is the 'sultan' – the ruling authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للإمام الذي ولاه الخليفة أن يقطع إنسانا من طريق الجادة إن لم يضر بالمارة، ومن أي يجوز للإمام جعله واليا من الإقطاع أن يعطي [؟] أن يقطع إلخ لأن للإمام ولاية التصرف في حق الكافة فيما فيه نظر للمسلمين، فإذا رأى في ذلك مصلحة لهم كان له أن يفعله من غير أن يلحق ضررا بأحد ألا ترى أنه إذا رأى أن يدخل بعض الطريق في المسجد أو بالعكس، وكان في ذلك مصلحة للمسلمين كان له أن يفعل ذلك، والإمام الذي ولاه الخليفة بمنزلة الخليفة؛ لأنه نائبه وهو الذي يسمى اليوم سلطانا. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٢٤/٩] من الجادة بالجيم وتشديد الدال المهملة معظم الطريق.
For the Imam appointed by the caliph, he may cut (allocate) a person from the main road if it does not harm the passersby. [Commentary:] 'He may cut' etc. because the Imam has authority to act in the interest of the general public regarding matters of benefit to Muslims. If he sees a benefit in doing so, he may do so without causing harm to anyone. Do you not see that if he decides to include part of the road into the mosque or vice versa, and there is benefit for Muslims in that, he may do so. And the Imam appointed by the caliph is like the caliph himself, because he is his deputy, and he is today called the sultan. Completion of al-Bahr al-Raiq: 9/424. 'Al-Jaddah' with jīm and shaddah on the dāl means the main road.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Imam (Abu Hanifa) regarding the authority of the Imam (ruler) over public roads. It states that the Imam may allocate a portion of the main road to an individual (e.g., for a building) provided it does not harm the public. The commentator explains that the Imam has general authority (wilāyat al-taṣarruf) over public interests. He may even alter the road for a mosque if it benefits Muslims. The appointed Imam (by the caliph) holds the same authority as the caliph. The term 'jāddah' is clarified as the main road. This is a clear example of the Hanafi principle that the ruler may act in the public interest (maṣlaḥa) within limits, without causing harm.

Key Terms
الجادة (al-jāddah): Main road or highway.
الإقطاع (al-iqṭāʿ): Allocation of land or property by the ruler to an individual.

C) He must only do it for a mosque.
D) He must get the consent of the person receiving it.

Key Takeaway
The Imam may allocate public road land only when it benefits the public and causes no harm, based on his authority and istihsan.

Sale Under Confiscation: Coercion in Cause vs. Act
Ruling on validity of sale after confiscation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between coercion in the act of sale and coercion in the underlying obligation, and apply the Hanafi ruling that sale under confiscation is valid when the sultan does not specify the sale.

Core Idea
A sale made under pressure to pay a demand is valid if the sale itself is voluntary—coercion in the cause does not invalidate the contract.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary addresses a practical scenario: a ruler confiscates property or demands a sum, and the owner sells his property to meet the demand. Is the sale valid? The Hanafi ruling draws a critical distinction between duress in the act of sale (coercion directed at the sale itself) and duress in the underlying obligation (the need to pay). The sale is valid unless the ruler explicitly orders the sale, making the sale itself coerced. An analogy with a debtor imprisoned by his creditor illustrates the principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling one's own property after the sultan has confiscated it (demanded money). The ruling: the sale is valid (sahih) if the sultan did not specify that the property must be sold.
The base ruling is from the matn; the commentary expands the condition.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is a qiyas (analogy) to the case of a debtor imprisoned for debt who sells property to pay off the debt. That sale is valid because the debtor sells voluntarily, even though he is under pressure to pay.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) because the illa is evident.
3
Analyze the illa (effective cause)
The illa is that the sale itself is voluntary; coercion exists only in the underlying obligation (the need to pay). In both cases (confiscation and debt), the owner/debtor chooses to sell, so the sale is not compelled.
Coercion (ikrāh) in Hanafi fiqh can be mulji' (overwhelming) or ghayr mulji' (lesser). Here it is not in the act.
4
Note the exception and its justification
If the sultan 'specifies the sale of his property' or orders him to sell, then the sale is invalid (unless the owner later willingly accepts the price). Because then the act itself becomes coerced—the sultan's command directly compels the sale.
This exception is explicitly mentioned to avoid misunderstanding the general ruling.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah)
The result: a sale made to meet a confiscation demand is valid in Hanafi law. This protects the buyer's ownership and allows the owner to free himself from the demand. The remedy is not to invalidate the sale but to address the unjust confiscation separately.
This contrasts with views that would void any transaction under governmental pressure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صادره السلطان ولم يعين بيع ماله فباع ماله صح أي بيعه؛ لأنه غير مكره به وإنما باع باختياره غاية الأمر أنه احتاج إلى بيعه لإيفاء ما طلب منه، وذلك لا يوجب الكره، كالدائن إذا حبس المديون بالدين فباع ماله ليقضي بثمنه دينه، فإنه يجوز؛ لأنه باعه باختياره، وإنما وقع الكره في الإيفاء لا في البيع. رمز الحقائق: ٥۰۹/۲] وإنما قيد بقوله : "لم يعين بيع ماله"؛ لأنه لو عين بيع ماله، أو أمره بالبيع فباعه مكرها، لا يصح إلا أن يأخذ الثمن طوعا. (عيني، ملا مسكين)
If the sultan confiscates his property and does not specify the sale of his property, then he sells his property, it is valid. [Commentary:] Meaning his sale is valid because he is not coerced to the sale; rather he sells by his own choice, the only matter being that he needed to sell it to fulfil what was demanded from him, and that does not create coercion. Like the case of a creditor who imprisons the debtor for debt, and the debtor sells his property to pay off his debt with its price, it is permissible because he sells by his choice. Coercion only occurred in the payment, not in the sale. Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/509. And he restricted his statement 'did not specify the sale of his property' because if the sultan had specified the sale of his property, or ordered him to sell, and he sells under coercion, it does not become valid unless he takes the price willingly. (Ayni, Mulla Miskin).
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi ruling regarding the validity of a sale when the owner's property has been confiscated by the sultan (ruler). If the sultan merely demands a sum of money (confiscation) without specifically ordering the sale of the owner's property, and the owner sells his property voluntarily to meet the demand, the sale is valid. The commentator explains that the sale itself is not coerced; coercion is only in the payment. An analogy is drawn with a debtor imprisoned by his creditor: the debtor sells his property to pay the debt, and the sale is valid because it is voluntary. However, if the sultan explicitly directs the sale or orders the owner to sell, then the sale is invalid under coercion (unless the owner willingly accepts the price). This ruling distinguishes between duress in the cause (payment) and duress in the act (sale).

Key Terms
المصادرة (al-muṣādara): Confiscation of property by the ruler or authority without specific legal cause.
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Coercion or duress that removes free choice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) If the sultan demands money and the owner sells his property.
B) If the sultan specifically orders the sale of the property.
C) If the owner sells willingly to pay the demand.
D) Always invalid because the owner is under duress.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a sale where the owner voluntarily sells to meet a confiscation demand, as long as the ruler does not compel the sale itself.

Ruling on Hawala and Subsequent Gift of Mahr
Ruling on validity of gift of mahr after assignment to a creditor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a wife's gift of her mahr after she has assigned it via hawala, and distinguish between specific and general assignment.

Core Idea
The right of the assignee attaches to the mahr, restricting the wife's disposal of it.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in Hanafi fiqh where a wife attempts to gift her mahr to her husband after assigning it to a creditor via hawala. The passage clarifies that the validity of the gift depends on the specificity of the assignment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the wife giving a gift of her mahr to her husband after she has already assigned (hawala) a creditor to collect that mahr from the husband. The ruling is that the gift is invalid (batil).
2
Understand the reason for invalidity
The creditor's right has attached to the mahr due to the specific assignment, analogous to a mortgage (rahn). Therefore, the wife cannot gift what is now effectively encumbered.
3
Distinguish specific vs general assignment
If the assignment was specific to the mahr (e.g., 'collect my mahr for your debt'), the gift is invalid. If the assignment was general (e.g., 'pay my debt from what is owed to me'), the gift is valid because no specific right attached.
4
Note the analogy to mortgage
The Hanafi school draws an analogy to a mortgaged property: just as a mortgagor cannot gift the mortgaged property without the mortgagee's consent, the wife cannot gift the mahr after specific hawala.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أحالت إنسانا على الزوج ثم وهبت المهر للزوج لا تصح. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٢٥/٩] صورة المسألة: أنه كان على المرأة دين لإنسان، فأحالت المرأة الدائن على زوجها بأن يقبض منه مهرها في دينه ثم وهبت المهر للزوج، لا تصح الهبة؛ لأنه تعلق به حق المحتال على مثال الرهن، وإن كان أسوة الغرماء عند موتها، فيرد تصرفها فيه، فصار كما باع المرهون أو وهبه هذا إذا أحالت بمهرها، ثم وهبت مهرها، أما إذا أطلقت الحوالة ثم وهبت المهر صح؛ إذ لا منافاة هذا حاصل ما في الشروح بتوضيح.
And if she assigns (by hawala) a person (her creditor) onto the husband (to collect her mahr), then she gifts the mahr to the husband, it is not valid. [Commentary:] Completion of al-Bahr al-Raiq: 9/425. The scenario of the issue: A woman owed a debt to a person. The woman assigned (hawala) the creditor to her husband to collect her mahr from him for his debt. Then she gifted the mahr to the husband. The gift is not valid because the right of the assignee (creditor) has attached to it, similar to a mortgage. Even though the assignee is only like other unsecured creditors in case of her death, this assignment restricts her disposal. It becomes like selling or gifting a mortgaged property. This applies if she assigned specifically the mahr. However, if she made a general assignment (without specifying the mahr) and then gifted the mahr, it is valid because there is no conflict. This is the gist of what is in the commentaries with clarification.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where a woman owes a debt to someone and she assigns (hawala) the creditor to her husband to collect her mahr (dower) from him to settle the debt. After this assignment, she tries to gift the same mahr to her husband. The ruling states that the gift is invalid. The reason is that the creditor's right has attached to that mahr, analogous to a mortgage. Therefore, the wife cannot dispose of it by gift. However, if she made a general assignment (e.g., assigning the debt without specifying the mahr), then gifting the mahr later is valid because no specific right attached. This ruling illustrates the Hanafi principle that a right of a third party (the assignee) can restrict the owner's disposal.

Key Terms
الحوالة (al-ḥawāla): Assignment of debt: transferring the right to collect a debt to a third party.
الرهن (al-rahn): Mortgage or pledge; a property held as collateral for a debt.

B) Never valid if she owes a debt.
C) Valid if the hawala was general and did not specify the mahr.
D) Valid if the husband agrees.

Key Takeaway
The attachment of a creditor's right via specific hawala restricts the wife's disposal of the mahr, making a subsequent gift invalid.

Liability for Snatching a Debtor from a Creditor
Ruling on liability for indirect interference with a debtor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of tasbīb (indirect causation) to determine when no liability arises due to an intervening free agent.

Core Idea
No liability for an indirect cause if a free agent directly causes the loss.

Imagine a creditor physically detaining a debtor to compel payment. A third party forcibly releases the debtor, who then escapes. Are you liable for the debt? The Hanafi school says no — but the situation is nuanced. This lesson unpacks the concept of tashbīb (indirect causation) and why the debtor's own free choice breaks the chain of liability. You will also see how the school analogizes this to untying a slave's shackle or pointing out property to a thief.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act: a third person (snatcher) forcibly removes a debtor from the creditor's physical hold, and the debtor then escapes. The ruling: the snatcher is not liable for the debt.
The ruling is about financial liability, not about the permissibility of the act itself.
2
Understand the concept of tashbīb
Tashbīb means indirect causation. The snatcher's act is a cause (sabab) but not the direct cause of the loss. The direct cause is the debtor's own act of escaping.
Hanafi law distinguishes between direct causation (mubāshara) and indirect causation (tashbīb).
3
Recognize the intervening free agent
The debtor is a free agent (mukhtār) who chooses to run away. His volitional act severs the causal link between the snatcher's act and the loss.
If the debtor had not escaped (e.g., was physically compelled), the ruling might differ.
4
Apply the analogy (qiyās)
The ruling is compared to untying a slave's shackle (the slave runs away voluntarily) or pointing out property to a thief (the thief steals voluntarily). In each, the immediate loss is caused by the free agent, not the indirect actor.
The effective cause (ʿilla) is the presence of an intervening free choice.
5
Note the taʿzīr component
Although the snatcher is not financially liable, the Imam may discipline him (taʿzīr) to deter such interference. This shows that the act itself is not permissible, even if it does not attract monetary compensation.
Taʿzīr is discretionary punishment for offenses without fixed hadd penalties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أخذ غريمه، فنزعه إنسان من يده لم يضمن، أي أخذ رجل غريمه، فنزعه إنسان من يده فهرب الغريم لم يضمن النازع؛ لأن النزع تسبيب، وقد وجد دخل فاعل مختار وهو هروبه فلا يضاف إليه التلف كما إذا حل قيد العبد فأبق، أو دل السارق على مال الغير؛ لأن التلف حصل بفعل السرقة لا بالدلالة، وكمن أمسك هاربا من عدوه حتى قتله، ولكن يعزره الإمام حتى لا يعود إلى مثله. فتح بتوضيح
If he seizes his debtor, and someone else snatches him from his hand, he (the snatcher) is not liable. [Commentary:] i.e., a man seizes his debtor, then a person snatches him from his hand, and the debtor escapes. The snatcher is not liable because the snatching is a mere cause (tasbīb), and there entered the act of a free agent, which is his escape. Therefore, the loss is not attributed to him. Just as if he unties the shackle of a slave and the slave runs away, or if he points out someone's property to a thief, because the loss occurs by the thief's act, not the pointing. Also like one who holds a person fleeing from his enemy until the enemy kills him. However, the Imam should discipline him so that he does not do it again. Fath, with clarification.
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage deals with a situation where a creditor physically holds his debtor (presumably to compel payment), and a third person forcibly releases the debtor, who then escapes. The ruling: the third person is not liable for the debt because his act is merely a cause (tashbīb), and the direct cause of loss (the debtor's escape) is the action of a free agent (the debtor himself). Analogies: untying a slave's shackle (the slave chooses to run away), or pointing out property to a thief (the thief steals, not the pointer). In all these, the intervening free choice breaks the chain of causation. However, the Imam may punish (ta'zīr) the offender to deter such interference.

Key Terms
التسبيب (al-tasbīb): Indirect causation where the direct cause is the action of a free agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Is a person who snatches a debtor from the creditor's hand liable for the debt if the debtor escapes?
A) Yes, because he caused the loss.
B) No, because the debtor's escape is a separate free act.
C) Yes, but only if he knew the debtor would escape.
D) No, because the creditor had no right to hold the debtor.

Key Takeaway
An indirect cause (tasbīb) does not create liability for a loss if a free agent directly causes it, though the indirect actor may be disciplined.

Liability for Indirect Damage: Tasbib vs. Ta'addi
Ruling on liability for well damage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on liability for damage caused by a well on one's property and identify the condition of ta'addi that triggers liability.

Core Idea
No liability for indirect damage (tasbib) from lawful property use; liability only for transgression (ta'addi).

This lesson explores a key Hanafi ruling on property rights and liability. When a well or cesspit on your land causes your neighbor's wall to collapse through seepage, are you liable? The answer hinges on a crucial distinction between permissible use and transgression. We'll unpack the legal reasoning behind this ruling and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Determine what action is being ruled upon and its legal outcome. The act is digging a well/cesspit on one's own property. The ruling: the owner is not forced to relocate and is not liable for damage unless he transgressed.
2
Locate the Proof (Dalil)
The proof is based on qiyas (analogical reasoning): a person using his own property lawfully is not liable for indirect harm (tasbib). The well owner acted within his rights, so no liability.
The source cites no specific text; the principle is established through legal maxims.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
How does the proof yield the ruling? Tasbib (indirect causation) without ta'addi does not impose liability. The exception is if the owner oversteps normal use (e.g., overwatering), which constitutes ta'addi and creates liability.
The example of placing a stone on a public road distinguishes lawful from transgressive acts.
4
Note the Scholarly Disagreement
Other schools may hold that foreseeable indirect harm can impose liability even without ta'addi. The Hanafi position is stricter, protecting property rights unless the owner is clearly at fault.
This ikhtilaf arises from differing interpretations of causation in fiqh.
5
Apply the Ruling to Similar Cases
Consider analogous situations: overwatering land, digging a basement that destabilizes a neighbor's foundation. The same principle applies — liability only if the owner's use exceeds normal bounds.
Practice applying this to modern scenarios like construction damage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتخذ بئرا في ملكه أو بالوعة فنز منها حائط جاره، وطلب تحويله لم يجبر عليه، فإن ثقب في وسط الدار سقط الحائط منه لم يضمن تحويله: تحويل ذلك إلى موضع آخر. لم يجبر عليه أي لم يجبر صاحب البئر والبالوعة على التحويل؛ لأنه تصرف في خالص ملكه ومفاد قوله: "لم يجبر" أنه يؤمر بالرفق دفعا للأذى. (عيني، فتح لم يضمن: أي صاحب البئر؛ لأن هذا تسبيب وبه لا يجب الضمان، إلا إذا كان متعديا كوضع الحجر على الطريق، واتخاذ ذلك المذكور من البئر وغيره في ملكه ليس بتعد، فلا يضمن تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٢٥/٩] قال في "الفتح": ومر في الإجارة: أنه لو سقى أرضه سقيا لا تحمله فتعدى الجاره ضمن
He dug a well or cesspit in his property, and from it the wall of his neighbor seeped, and he asked to relocate it: he is not forced to do so. Then if a hole [formed] in the middle of the house, and the wall fell from it, he is not liable. Its relocation: relocation to another place. He is not forced: i.e., the owner of the well and cesspit is not forced to relocate, because it is an act in his exclusive property. And the implication of his saying 'he is not forced' is that he is instructed to be gentle to avoid harm. (Ayni, Fath) He is not liable: i.e., the owner of the well, because this is tasbib and with it liability is not obligatory, unless he was transgressing, like placing a stone on the road. And making the aforementioned well and other things on his property is not transgression, so he does not guarantee. Completion of al-Bahr al-Raiq: 9/425. He said in al-Fath: and it passed in Ijarah that if he watered his land with irrigation that it cannot bear, and it transgressed to the neighbor, he guarantees.
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on liability for damage caused by a well or cesspit on one's own property. The base ruling states that the owner is neither forced to relocate his well nor liable for damage to the neighbor's wall caused by seepage, as long as he was not negligent. The commentary explains the key concept of tasbib (indirect causation): since the wall fell due to the seepage, but the well owner acted within his rights, liability does not attach. However, if he exceeded normal use—such as overwatering his land—he becomes a transgressor and must compensate. The passage also notes that the judge instructs the owner to be courteous to prevent harm, but cannot compel removal.

Key Terms
تسبيب (tasbib): Indirect causation where the harm results through an intermediary but the original actor's action was lawful.
تعدي (ta'addi): Transgression beyond the normal permitted use of one's property, such as overwatering.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is required for a person to be liable for damage caused by a well on his property to his neighbor's wall?
A) The existence of damage alone
B) The neighbor's request for removal
C) The well owner's transgression (ta'addi)
D) The wall falling within three days

Key Takeaway
Under Hanafi law, an owner is not liable for indirect damage from a lawful use of his property unless he transgresses the norm (ta'addi).

Lending Orphan's Property: The Judge's Authority and Exceptions
Ruling on who may lend orphan's property and conditions for the finder

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify who is permitted to lend an orphan's property according to the Hanafi school and explain the condition under which the finder of lost property may lend.

Core Idea
Only the judge may lend orphan's property; the finder may lend only after the announcement period passes.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies a key distinction in the management of orphan property: while the judge has full authority to lend orphan funds with a written deed, other guardians like the father and executor do not. However, there is a special exception for the finder of lost property after the announcement period has elapsed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling
The judge has the authority to lend the orphan's property and must document the loan with a written deed (sakk). This is the general rule.
The judge acts as the official guardian of orphans under his jurisdiction.
2
Identify the excluded parties and reason
The father, executor (wasi), and finder (multaqit) are not permitted to lend orphan property because they lack the ability to sue for repayment, leading to potential waste of the orphan's funds.
The passage uses 'aajiza 'an (inability), possibly referring to inability to enforce repayment.
3
Examine the exception for the finder
After the finder announces the lost property and the prescribed announcement period expires without the owner appearing, the finder may lend the property to the poor. This is because giving charity in that situation is permissible, and lending is even more virtuous.
The finder's authority is conditional and limited to lending to the poor, not to others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقرض القاضي مال اليتيم ويكتب الصك بخلاف الأب والوصي والملتقط؛ لأنهم عاجزون عن فيكون تضييعا إلا أن الملتقط إذا أنشد اللقطة ومضى مدة النشد ينبغي أن يجوز له الإقراض من الفقراء؛ لأنه لو تصدق به عليهم في هذه الحالة جاز فالقرض أولى.
And the judge lends the orphan's property and writes a deed, contrary to the father, executor, and finder, because they are incapable of [suing?] so it would be waste. Except that the finder, if he announces the lost property and the period of announcement passes, it is permissible for him to lend to the poor, because if he gives charity to them in this situation, it is permissible, so lending is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on lending orphan's property. The judge has the authority to lend the orphan's money and document it in a written contract. The father (even if a guardian), the executor (wasi), and the finder of lost property (multaqit) cannot lend out orphan's money because they lack the ability to sue for repayment, which would result in loss. However, the finder of lost property, after the period of announcement (nashad) has passed, may lend the lost item to the poor if the owner does not appear, because giving charity to the poor is permissible in that state, and lending is even better than charity as it may be returned.

Key Terms
القاضي (al-qadi): The judge who has authority to manage the orphan's property and lend it.
الصك (al-sakk): A written deed or document evidencing the loan.
الملتقط (al-multaqit): The finder of lost property (luqatah).
الأب (al-ab): The father of the orphan; natural guardian but not permitted to lend orphan property.
الوصي (al-wasi): The executor appointed in a will to manage the orphan's affairs; also not permitted to lend.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After the announcement period, the finder may lend the lost property to the poor because:
A) Lending is more appropriate than charity
B) He becomes the owner after announcement
C) The judge permits it
D) It is a mandatory duty

Key Takeaway
The judge alone has general authority to lend orphan property; the finder has limited authority only after the announcement period and only to the poor.
